reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a depthwise-separable 1D convolution: a depthwise Conv1d (each channel independently)
    followed by a pointwise Conv1d (1x1, mixes channels). Used in Enformer and genomic sequence
    models for efficient spatial+channel factorized processing.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the pointwise convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Kernel size of the depthwise convolution.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the depthwise convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding for the depthwise convolution. Defaults to 0.
        bias (bool, optional): If True, adds a learnable bias. Defaults to False.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.depthwise = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, in_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, groups=in_channels, bias=bias)
        self.pointwise = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, bias=bias)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        x = self.depthwise(x)
        x = self.pointwise(x)
        return x

batch_size = 32
in_channels = 256
out_channels = 512
kernel_size = 9
seq_len = 4096
stride = 1
padding = 4

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, in_channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for a depthwise separable 1D convolution: depthwise conv1d (groups=in_channels) then pointwise conv1d (1x1). Input shape: [batch_size, in_channels, seq_len]. Depthwise conv: each channel independently, kernel size K, stride S, padding P. Output of depthwise: [batch_size, in_channels, out_len] where out_len = floor((seq_len + 2*P - K)/S)+1. Since stride=1, padding=4, kernel_size=9, out_len = seq_len + 8 - 9 + 1 = seq_len = 4096. Then pointwise conv: 1x1, mixing channels: out_channels x in_channels x 1. Bias possible. The reference uses bias in both depthwise and pointwise if bias=True. We need to handle generic hyperparameters? The constructor arguments are same as model, but presumably test will use same values or potentially different ones. Need generic implementation. Goal: faster than PyTorch reference. PyTorch's reference likely does two separate conv1d calls via cudnn. Since depthwise conv1d is memory-bound, and pointwise conv1d is essentially a GEMM over channels (small spatial dimension? Actually spatial=4096, channels=256->512). Could fuse them into a single kernel: compute depthwise on the fly and accumulate into pointwise output. This saves intermediate write/read of depthwise output. Fusing should provide speedup. We need to write custom CUDA kernel using load_inline. Must be careful with batch size, in_channels, out_channels, seq_len, kernel_size, stride, padding, bias. Let's compute dimension formulas: For input x of shape [B, C, L], depthwise weights w_d of shape [C, 1, K], bias_d of shape [C] if bias else none. Depthwise output y_d shape [B, C, L_out], L_out = (L + 2*P - K) // S + 1. Pointwise weights w_p of shape [D, C, 1], bias_p of shape [D] if bias else none. Output y shape [B, D, L_out]. We can fuse: for each output position (b, d, l_out), compute sum_c w_p[d,c,0] * sum_{k=0}^{K-1} w_d[c,0,k] * x[b, c, l_in], where l_in = l_out*S + k - P (with appropriate zero padding). So overall: y[b, d, l] = bias_p[d] + sum_c w_p[d,c] * (bias_d[c] + sum_{k} w_d[c,k] * x[b,c,l*S+k-P]) We can rearrange: y[b,d,l] = bias_p[d] + sum_c w_p[d,c] * bias_d[c] + sum_c w_p[d,c] * sum_k w_d[c,k] * x[b,c,l*S + k - P] If we precompute combined bias: bias_out[d] = bias_p[d] + sum_c w_p[d,c] * bias_d[c] (if both bias exist). Within block: - blockDim.x = D_TILE (e.g., 64) - blockDim.y = L_TILE (e.g., 16) - blockDim = (D_TILE, L_TILE). Total threads = 1024. Shared memory: - `smem_M[C * L_TILE]` (or maybe smaller if we tile C). But C can be up to e.g., 256 or more. Better: tile over C in chunks of C_TILE (e.g., 32 or 64). Process C in chunks: For each C chunk [c_start, c_start + C_TILE): 1. Compute M[l] for c in chunk and l in L_TILE. Store in smem of size C_TILE * L_TILE. 2. Each thread computes partial accumulation over c in chunk: y_partial += sum_{c in chunk} w_p[d,c] * M[c,l]. 3. Move to next chunk. This limits shared memory usage: C_TILE * L_TILE floats. Choose C_TILE and L_TILE to fit. For typical C=256, C_TILE=32, L_TILE=32 => 32*32*4 = 4KB. Very comfortable. So total algorithm per block: ``` for b_id = blockIdx.z: for tile_d = blockIdx.x * D_TILE: for tile_l = blockIdx.y * L_TILE: float acc = 0.0f; for c_start = 0; c_start < C; c_start += C_TILE: __syncthreads(); // maybe at start of loop // Compute depthwise partial M for this chunk int tid = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_idx = tid / L_TILE; int l_idx = tid % L_TILE; if (c_idx < C_TILE && c_start + c_idx < C) { int c = c_start + c_idx; int l_out = tile_l + l_idx; float m = bias_d[c]; // if bias, else 0 for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int l_in = l_out * stride + k - padding; float xval = 0.0f; if (l_in >= 0 && l_in < L_in) { xval = x[ ((b_id * C + c) * L_in + l_in) ]; } m += w_d[c * K + k] * xval; } smem[l_idx * C_TILE + c_idx] = m; } else { smem[l_idx * C_TILE + c_idx] = 0.0f; // padding if c out of bounds } __syncthreads(); // Compute pointwise partial for assigned (d,l) int d = tile_d + threadIdx.x; int l_out = tile_l + threadIdx.y; if (d < D && l_out < L_out) { float local_acc = 0.0f; int c_limit = min(C_TILE, C - c_start); for (int c = 0; c < c_limit; ++c) { float m = smem[l_out - tile_l) * C_TILE + c]; // l_out - tile_l = threadIdx.y float w = w_p[(c_start + c) * D + d]; // weight layout depends local_acc += w * m; } acc += local_acc; } __syncthreads(); // before next chunk reused smem } // After all chunks if (d < D && l_out < L_out) { if (pointwise_bias) acc += bias_p[d]; y[ ((b_id * D + d) * L_out + l_out) ] = acc; } ``` Wait weight layout: In PyTorch, Conv1d weight is [out_channels, in_channels/groups, kernel_size]. But we should also support arbitrary values. Our kernel with constants D_TILE=32, L_TILE=32, C_TILE=32 should work for any sizes, with bounds checking. One performance concern: For small D or L, the tiling might have many idle threads. But not an issue for evaluation. Now, let's implement the kernel in C++ and wrap it with pybind11-compatible function. Use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. We should also consider using `at::Tensor` in C++ wrapper. The wrapper function signature: `torch::Tensor forward(const torch::Tensor& x, const torch::Tensor& w_d, const torch::Tensor& b_d, const torch::Tensor& w_p_t, const torch::Tensor& b_p, int stride, int padding)`. It returns output tensor. But `load_inline` auto-generates pybind11 wrappers for the functions we list. So we just declare a function taking tensors and returning tensor. Let's check typical usage: ```python cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor forward(...); PYBIND11_MODULE(... ) { ... } // but load_inline auto-generates """ ``` Actually we are told: "Do not write your own `PYBIND11_MODULE(...)` block in `cpp_sources`. `load_inline` auto-generates one from the `functions=[...]` argument; including your own causes a duplicate-symbol redefinition error at compile time. And `functions=["forward"]`. But the C++ wrapper needs to call the CUDA kernel, which is defined in `cuda_sources`. Or we can put the wrapper in `cpp_sources` and use `AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES` etc. Let's design the code. C++ wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor forward(const torch::Tensor& x, const torch::Tensor& w_d, const torch::Tensor& b_d, const torch::Tensor& w_p_t, // transposed pointwise weight [C, D] const torch::Tensor& b_p, int stride, int padding) { // check contiguous, float auto B = x.size(0); auto C = x.size(1); auto L_in = x.size(2); auto K = w_d.size(2); // w_d shape [C,1,K] auto D = w_p_t.size(1); // w_p_t shape [C,D] auto L_out = (L_in + 2 * padding - K) / stride + 1; auto y = torch::empty({B, D, L_out}, x.options()); // Launch kernel const int D_TILE = 32; const int L_TILE = 32; const int C_TILE = 32; dim3 block(D_TILE, L_TILE); dim3 grid((D + D_TILE - 1) / D_TILE, (L_out + L_TILE - 1) / L_TILE, B); fused_conv1d_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), w_d.data_ptr<float>(), b_d.defined() && b_d.numel() > 0 ? b_d.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, w_p_t.data_ptr<float>(), b_p.defined() && b_p.numel() > 0 ? b_p.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, B, C, D, L_in, L_out, K, stride, padding, y.data_ptr<float>() ); return y; } ``` But note: if bias=False, we will pass empty tensors. Need to check empty. In PyTorch, `nn.Conv1d` bias is None if bias=False. But in our `ModelNew`, we can store the modules and in `forward` call the kernel. We need to handle `bias=False` in the kernel argument. We can pass a bool flag. Alternatively, we can just precompute combined bias on CPU and pass a single bias vector or null. That might simplify the kernel. Let's think: depthwise bias adds to each channel before pointwise. We can just compute M = sum_k w_d*x + (bias_d if exists). In the kernel, we can check `bias_d != nullptr`. That's easy. Pointwise bias adds after pointwise sum. Check `bias_p != nullptr`. So wrapper passes pointers and maybe bools. Or we can overload. Since C++ wrapper is compiled with standard C++, using `const float*` and passing `nullptr` if no bias is fine. But we need to ensure that when bias=False, the pointer is null. In Python forward, we can pass `self.depthwise.bias if self.depthwise.bias is not None else torch.empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype)` or just pass `self.depthwise.bias` which is None. But the C++ function signature expects `const torch::Tensor&`. Passing a None tensor to C++ creates an undefined tensor. We can use `b_d` and check `b_d.defined()` and `b_d.numel() > 0`. Similarly, if we pass a tensor, we can get its data_ptr. If undefined, `data_ptr()` may crash. So we must check `defined()`. Alternatively, our Python forward can preprocess: if bias exists, pass it; else pass a zero tensor or empty. Actually passing a zero tensor of shape [C] is easiest: then the kernel just adds zero. But that adds an extra read. Since the kernel is memory bound, maybe negligible. But we can just check `bias_d != nullptr`. Let's have Python forward prepare the tensors: ```python b_d = self.depthwise.bias if self.depthwise.bias is not None else torch.empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) b_p = self.pointwise.bias if self.pointwise.bias is not None else torch.empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) ``` In C++ wrapper: ``` bool has_bias_d = b_d.defined() && b_d.numel() > 0; bool has_bias_p = b_p.defined() && b_p.numel() > 0; ``` Then pass pointers: ``` const float* bias_d_ptr = has_bias_d ? b_d.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr; const float* bias_p_ptr = has_bias_p ? b_p.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr; ``` Kernel signature: ``` __global__ void fused_conv1d_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w_d, const float* __restrict__ bias_d, const float* __restrict__ w_p_t, const float* __restrict__ bias_p, int B, int C, int D, int L_in, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, float* __restrict__ y) { // ... } ``` In kernel: ``` float bias_d_val = (bias_d != nullptr) ? bias_d[c] : 0.0f; ``` Then add. Now, one more thing: transposed pointwise weight. In Python: ``` w_p = self.pointwise.weight.squeeze(2).t().contiguous() ``` Since `self.pointwise.weight` shape is [D, C, 1], `.squeeze(2)` -> [D, C], `.t()` -> [C, D]. `.contiguous()` ensures row-major with C leading, D contiguous. Depthwise weight: `w_d = self.depthwise.weight.squeeze(1).contiguous()` -> shape [C, K] (since original [C, 1, K]). Input x is already contiguous? We can call `.contiguous()` in forward for safety. `x = x.contiguous()`. Now, let's implement the kernel carefully. Kernel code: ``` #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <torch/extension.h> #define D_TILE 32 #define L_TILE 32 #define C_TILE 32 __global__ void fused_conv1d_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w_d, const float* __restrict__ bias_d, const float* __restrict__ w_p_t, const float* __restrict__ bias_p, int B, int C, int D, int L_in, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, float* __restrict__ y) { __shared__ float smem[L_TILE * C_TILE]; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_d = blockIdx.x * D_TILE; int tile_l = blockIdx.y * L_TILE; int thread_x = threadIdx.x; // 0..D_TILE-1 int thread_y = threadIdx.y; // 0..L_TILE-1 float acc = 0.0f; // Precompute base output indices int d = tile_d + thread_x; int l_out = tile_l + thread_y; for (int c_start = 0; c_start < C; c_start += C_TILE) { // Phase 1: compute depthwise partial M for this chunk int tid = thread_y * D_TILE + thread_x; // 0..1023 int c_idx = tid / L_TILE; int l_idx = tid % L_TILE; int c = c_start + c_idx; int l_out_m = tile_l + l_idx; float m_val = 0.0f; if (c < C && l_out_m < L_out) { if (bias_d != nullptr) { m_val = bias_d[c]; } // pointer to start of this channel's input const float* x_ptr = x + ((b * C + c) * L_in); const float* w_ptr = w_d + (c * K); #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int l_in = l_out_m * stride + k - padding; float x_val = 0.0f; if (l_in >= 0 && l_in < L_in) { x_val = x_ptr[l_in]; } m_val += w_ptr[k] * x_val; } } smem[l_idx * C_TILE + c_idx] = m_val; __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: accumulate pointwise for assigned (d, l_out) if (d < D && l_out < L_out) { int c_limit = min(C_TILE, C - c_start); // smem row for this l_out const float* smem_row = smem + (thread_y * C_TILE); const float* w_ptr = w_p_t + (c_start * D + d); // w_p_t is [C, D] contiguous // for c offset i, weight is at (c_start + i)*D + d // So w_ptr points to weight for c_start, d. // Then w_ptr[i*D] for i=0..c_limit-1 #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < c_limit; ++i) { float m = smem_row[i]; float w = w_ptr[i * D]; acc += w * m; } } __syncthreads(); } if (d < D && l_out < L_out) { if (bias_p != nullptr) { acc += bias_p[d]; } y[((b * D + d) * L_out + l_out)] = acc; } } ``` Wait, weight indexing: w_p_t is [C, D] contiguous. So element (c, d) is at `c * D + d`. For a block, we need to read for c_start + i, d. So pointer = `w_p_t + (c_start + i) * D + d`. If we set `w_ptr = w_p_t + c_start * D + d`, then `w_ptr[i * D]` gets the correct weight. Good. In the inner loop, we do `w_ptr[i * D]`. But D can be up to 512, which is a stride. But we only have one thread reading per i; each thread reads from memory. Since threads in a warp have same `d`? Wait no, `d = tile_d + thread_x`. Threads in a warp have varying thread_x, thus varying d. But they read weight for same c_start + i and varying d. Since weight memory is arranged as (c_start + i)*D + d, consecutive d are contiguous. So threads in a warp reading varying d but same (c_start + i) access contiguous memory. That is coalesced. The address is `w_p_t + (c_start + i) * D + d`. So `w_ptr` offset depends on `d`, which is `tile_d + thread_x`. Since different warps have different `d`, coalescing holds. In the loop, `w_ptr[i * D]` is accessed with a stride of `D` between iterations. That's fine because each thread reads sequentially. Now, for M computation: `x_ptr = x + ((b * C + c) * L_in)`. Then `x_ptr[l_in]`. Since threads in warp have varying l_idx (because `tid % L_TILE`, and if L_TILE=32, warp consists of threads with same c_idx and varying l_idx). Actually `tid = thread_y * D_TILE + thread_x`. Since D_TILE=32, `tid` goes from 0 to 1023. For warp size 32, warp 0 has tid 0..31. `c_idx = tid / 32 = 0`, `l_idx = tid % 32 = 0..31`. So warp 0 has c_idx=0, l_idx varying. Good. Warp 1 has c_idx=1, l_idx varying. So each warp handles one c and all l. Memory access: for fixed c and varying l_in, `x_ptr[l_in]` is contiguous across warp. Perfect. For weight w_d: `w_ptr = w_d + c * K`. All threads in warp read same c, so same base pointer. Each thread reads same `w_ptr[k]` for each k. Broadcast. So the M phase is well coalesced. One optimization: In M phase, for a given l_out_m, we compute `l_in = l_out_m * stride + k - padding`. If stride=1, padding=4, K=9, l_in ranges over l_out_m -4 .. l_out_m +4. For threads in a warp, l_out_m varies, so l_in varies. The bounds check `if (l_in >= 0 && l_in < L_in)` is branchy but K is small. We can optimize by precomputing for the whole block whether the entire tile is within bounds. For padding=4 and L_in=4096, and tile_l from 0 to 4095 with L_TILE=32, most tiles are fully inside. Only the first tile (tile_l=0) has some l_out < 4, causing some l_in negative. Similarly last tile might exceed L_in. We could handle with a boundary check block-level to avoid per-thread branching, but per-thread branch on small K is cheap. The compiler may use predication. Given K is small, per-thread branch is fine. One more thing: The weight `w_d` pointer indexing: In PyTorch, depthwise weight shape is [C, 1, K], squeezed to [C, K]. So `w_d[c * K + k]`. Good. The bias_d pointer indexing: `bias_d[c]`. Good. Now, the output writing: `y[((b * D + d) * L_out + l_out)]`. For a warp, b and d same? Actually in our block mapping, threads in a warp have same thread_y (l), but varying thread_x (d). Wait blockDim.x = D_TILE = 32, blockDim.y = L_TILE = 32. threadIdx.x varies d, threadIdx.y varies l. In CUDA, threads with consecutive threadIdx.x are in same warp (since threadIdx.x is fastest). So warp consists of 32 threads with same threadIdx.y (same l) and threadIdx.x = 0..31 (varying d). So for a given warp, l_out is constant, d varies. Then writing to `y[((b * D + d) * L_out + l_out)]`. For fixed b,l, varying d, addresses are separated by L_out. That's a stride of L_out (4096). So writes are strided, not contiguous. That's bad for coalescing! We need to fix this. Oh no! The output should be coalesced when writing contiguous memory. For output layout [B, D, L_out], varying d means addresses: `base + d * L_out + l_out`. That's a stride of L_out. So within a warp, threads write to memory locations separated by 4096 floats = 16KB. That's not coalesced. In fact, each thread writes to a different cache line. So 32 threads cause 32 memory transactions. That's terrible. Similarly, for M computation, we had varying l across warp, which accesses contiguous x. That's good. But for output writing and pointwise weight reading, varying d across warp reads weights with stride 1 (contiguous) for a fixed c, but writes to output with stride L_out. To fix output coalescing, we should transpose the thread assignment: have threadIdx.x vary over L (contiguous) and threadIdx.y vary over D (strided). But then pointwise weight access for varying D is strided? Let's see. If we map block as: - blockDim.x = L_TILE (contiguous over L) - blockDim.y = D_TILE (over D) Then warp (32 threads) has varying L and same D. For output writing: addresses `base + d * L_out + l_out`. Since l_out varies, addresses are contiguous! That's coalesced. Good. For pointwise weight reading: for a fixed c and varying d across blockDim.y, different warps have different d. But within a warp, d is constant. So when reading weight for a given c, all threads in warp read the same weight address (broadcast). That's fine, no coalescing needed. For M computation: we need to compute M for varying c and l. With blockDim.x = L_TILE, blockDim.y = D_TILE. But M computation doesn't depend on D. We can still use the same linearization `tid = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x`. Then `c_idx = tid / L_TILE`, `l_idx = tid % L_TILE`. With blockDim.x = L_TILE = 32, `l_idx = tid % 32 = threadIdx.x` (since threadIdx.y * 32 + threadIdx.x, modulo 32 = threadIdx.x). So l_idx = threadIdx.x. c_idx = threadIdx.y + (blockIdx.x? no). Actually `tid / 32 = threadIdx.y`. So `c_idx = threadIdx.y`. That means each thread's c_idx is determined by threadIdx.y (0..D_TILE-1). But D_TILE might be 32, c_idx ranges 0..31. So threads with same threadIdx.y have same c_idx, and varying l_idx=threadIdx.x. So warp consists of threads with same threadIdx.y (same c_idx) and varying threadIdx.x (varying l). That's exactly what we want for M computation: varying l across warp, contiguous x reads. Good! But wait: D_TILE (blockDim.y) is 32, which defines the number of c_idx values we can map to. The mapping uses `tid` up to D_TILE * L_TILE = 1024. c_idx = tid / L_TILE = 0..31. So we have 32 channels per chunk. That matches C_TILE=32. Perfect! Then for pointwise accumulation, each thread is assigned to (d=blockIdx.x*D_TILE + threadIdx.y, l=blockIdx.y*L_TILE + threadIdx.x). So threadIdx.x determines l, threadIdx.y determines d. This means within a warp, l varies and d is constant. Output writes are contiguous. Weight reads for varying c but fixed d are broadcast across warp (same address). But reading M from smem: M is stored as `smem[l_idx * C_TILE + c_idx]`. In the new mapping, `l_idx = threadIdx.x`, `c_idx = ???`. Wait when reading M for pointwise, each thread needs M for its l (threadIdx.x) and all c in chunk. So M row index = threadIdx.x. The column index c varies across loop. In smem, M is stored `smem[l_idx * C_TILE + c_idx]`. For reading, `smem[threadIdx.x * C_TILE + c]`. Since threadIdx.x varies across warp, each thread reads a different row. But within a warp, they read the same c across threads? No, they read varying c as loop goes. For a fixed c, threads in warp read `smem[threadIdx.x * C_TILE + c]`. Since threadIdx.x varies, they read contiguous elements in smem (since fixed c, varying row). Shared memory handles this with no bank conflicts if C_TILE is 32: `addr = threadIdx.x * 32 + c`. Bank = (addr % 32) = c % 32. So all threads in warp read same bank? That would be a bank conflict! Wait bank conflicts occur when multiple threads in a warp access different addresses that map to the same bank. With 32 banks, address % 32 determines bank. `addr = threadIdx.x * 32 + c`. Since `threadIdx.x * 32` is a multiple of 32, `addr % 32 = c % 32`. So for fixed c, all threads in warp access the same bank! That's a 32-way bank conflict. That's terrible. We need to store smem in a bank-conflict-free layout for the pointwise read pattern. Previously, with blockDim.x = D_TILE, blockDim.y = L_TILE, smem layout was `smem[l_idx * C_TILE + c_idx]`. For pointwise read, thread (d,l) reads `smem[l * C_TILE + c]`. Since l is threadIdx.y, if blockDim.x = D_TILE=32, warp threads have same l (threadIdx.y constant), varying d. Then they read same `l` and varying `c` across loop? Wait no, they all read the same `l`, but for a fixed c they all read exactly the same address! That's a broadcast, no conflict. But the write pattern had varying l across warp. The read pattern had varying c but same l (broadcast). So no bank conflict. However, the output write was strided. Now we swapped to fix output writes. But we introduced bank conflicts in smem reads. Let's analyze carefully. Option 1: Keep original block mapping (threadIdx.x = d, threadIdx.y = l). Output writes strided. To fix output writes, we could transpose the output tensor on host or in shared memory. But that adds complexity. Option 2: Swap mapping (threadIdx.x = l, threadIdx.y = d). Fix output writes but introduce smem bank conflicts on read. Can we restructure smem to avoid bank conflicts for this read pattern? We need to store M such that for fixed c and varying l, accesses are bank-conflict free. Since in pointwise phase, threads with varying l read M for same c. That is, we need to read `M[l][c]` for varying l. In shared memory, we should store M with c as the fast dimension and l as slow? Let's think. Bank conflict free access for varying l and fixed c: we want addresses `addr(l) = f(l, c)` such that `addr(l) % 32` varies with l. If we store row-major with l leading: `addr = l * C_TILE + c`. Then `addr % 32 = (l * C_TILE + c) % 32`. If C_TILE is 32, `addr % 32 = c % 32`, constant across l -> conflict. If C_TILE is e.g., 33 (not power of two), `addr % 32 = (l*33 + c) % 32 = (l + c) % 32`. For varying l, bank varies -> no conflict! So we can pad C_TILE to 33. But C_TILE is compile-time constant. We could define `smem[L_TILE][C_TILE + 1]` or `smem[L_TILE * (C_TILE + 1)]`. That would avoid bank conflicts for row-major with l leading. Let's check: `addr = l * (C_TILE + 1) + c`. Then `(l * 33 + c) % 32 = (l + c) % 32`. Since l varies across threads in warp, banks vary -> no conflict. Good. Similarly, when writing M in phase 1, we assign threads such that each thread writes one (c,l). With linear mapping `tid = threadIdx.y * L_TILE + threadIdx.x` (since blockDim.x = L_TILE, blockDim.y = D_TILE). Then `c_idx = tid / L_TILE`, `l_idx = tid % L_TILE`. So each thread writes `smem[l_idx * (C_TILE+1) + c_idx]`. For a warp, l_idx varies? Actually if D_TILE=32, L_TILE=32, and blockDim.x=32 (l), blockDim.y=32 (d), then `tid = d*32 + l`. Warp comprises 32 threads with same d and varying l (since d increments every 32 threads). So warp has constant c_idx = d, varying l_idx = l. Writes are to `addr = l * 33 + c`. Since c is constant across warp, `addr % 32 = (l + c) % 32`, varying with l. So writes are also bank-conflict free. Perfect! So if we use `threadIdx.x` for L, `threadIdx.y` for D, and store smem with pitch `C_TILE + 1`, we get bank-conflict-free accesses for both phases. And output writes are contiguous. Let's verify all memory access patterns with this mapping: Block dimensions: `blockDim.x = L_TILE = 32`, `blockDim.y = D_TILE = 32`. Grid: `gridDim.x = ceil(D / D_TILE)`, `gridDim.y = ceil(L_out / L_TILE)`, `gridDim.z = B`. Thread mapping: - `l = blockIdx.y * L_TILE + threadIdx.x` - `d = blockIdx.x * D_TILE + threadIdx.y` Phase 1: compute M for chunk. Linear tid = `threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x` = `d_local * 32 + l_local`. - `c_idx = tid / L_TILE = d_local` (since L_TILE=32). Because `d_local*32 + l_local` divided by 32 = d_local. - `l_idx = tid % L_TILE = l_local`. So each thread computes M for `c = c_start + d_local`, `l_out = tile_l + l_local`. Warp: 32 threads with same d_local (c_idx) and varying l_local. x reads: for fixed c, varying l_out, contiguous in memory. Coalesced. w_d reads: same c, broadcast. smem writes: `smem[l_local * (C_TILE + 1) + d_local]`. Since warp has varying l_local and same d_local, addresses are `l*33 + c`, banks = (l + c) % 32, varying. No bank conflict. Good. Phase 2: pointwise accumulation. Each thread loops c from 0 to c_limit-1. - Reads M: `smem[l_local * (C_TILE + 1) + c]`. - Warp has same d_local, varying l_local. - For a given c, addresses = `l*33 + c`, banks = (l+c)%32, varying. No bank conflict. Good. - Reads weight: `w_p_t[(c_start + c) * D + d]`. - d = blockIdx.x*D_TILE + d_local. Varying d_local across warp (since warp has varying d_local? Wait warp: threads with same d_local? Let's see: `tid = d_local * 32 + l_local`. A warp is 32 consecutive tids. Since l_local ranges 0..31, a warp has fixed d_local and varying l_local. So in this mapping, a warp has constant d_local and varying l_local. Since threadIdx.y = d_local, and warp is organized along threadIdx.x first, then threadIdx.y? Actually warp assignment: threads are ordered by threadIdx.x (fastest), then threadIdx.y, then threadIdx.z. So a warp of 32 threads will have threadIdx.x = 0..31 and threadIdx.y = 0. So d_local = 0 for warp 0, d_local = 1 for warp 1, etc. So within a warp, d_local is constant. Thus all threads in a warp have the same d (output channel). Good. - Then weight read `w_p_t[(c_start + c) * D + d]`: same d across warp, same c in a loop iteration. So all threads read the same address. Broadcast. No bank issue. - Output writes: `y[((b * D + d) * L_out + l)]`. - d constant across warp, l varying. For fixed b,d, varying l writes contiguous memory. Perfect coalescing. This mapping is ideal! So blockDim.x = L_TILE = 32, blockDim.y = D_TILE = 32. But we need to compute `c_idx = tid / L_TILE`. Since L_TILE is compile-time constant 32, division by 32 is shift. Good. Now we need to define `C_TILE + 1` padded shared memory. The size is `L_TILE * (C_TILE + 1)` floats = 32 * 33 * 4 = 4352 bytes. Well within limits. One caveat: In phase 1, some threads have `c = c_start + d_local` which may be >= C. In that case, we write 0.0f. Since d_local ranges 0..31, and c_start increments by 32, for the last chunk, d_local may exceed remaining channels. We write 0.0f. Good. Similarly, l_local may exceed L_out if L_out not multiple of 32. In that case, we write 0.0f. Good. In phase 2, threads with d >= D or l >= L_out skip accumulation and final write. Since d is based on blockIdx.x * 32 + d_local, for the last D tile, some d_local may exceed D. The `if (d < D && l < L_out)` guard handles it. Now, we need to ensure all threads call `__syncthreads()`. In phase 2, threads that skip accumulation still need to call `__syncthreads()` at the end. Since they are inside the `c_start` loop, we should put `__syncthreads()` after the `if (d < D && l < L_out)` block, unconditionally. But CUDA `__syncthreads()` requires all threads in block to reach it. Since the `if` block doesn't include the syncthreads, it's fine. Good. Let's revise the kernel code with this mapping. ``` __global__ void fused_conv1d_kernel(...) { const int D_TILE = 32; const int L_TILE = 32; const int C_TILE = 32; const int SMEM_PITCH = C_TILE + 1; __shared__ float smem[L_TILE * SMEM_PITCH]; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_d = blockIdx.x * D_TILE; int tile_l = blockIdx.y * L_TILE; int l_local = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int d_local = threadIdx.y; // 0..31 int d = tile_d + d_local; int l = tile_l + l_local; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_start = 0; c_start < C; c_start += C_TILE) { // Phase 1 int tid = d_local * L_TILE + l_local; int c_idx = tid / L_TILE; // = d_local int l_idx = tid % L_TILE; // = l_local // Since L_TILE=32, these simplify. int c = c_start + c_idx; int l_m = tile_l + l_idx; float m_val = 0.0f; if (c < C && l_m < L_out) { if (bias_d != nullptr) m_val = bias_d[c]; const float* x_ptr = x + ((b * C + c) * L_in); const float* w_ptr = w_d + (c * K); for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int l_in = l_m * stride + k - padding; float xv = 0.0f; if (l_in >= 0 && l_in < L_in) { xv = x_ptr[l_in]; } m_val += w_ptr[k] * xv; } } smem[l_idx * SMEM_PITCH + c_idx] = m_val; __syncthreads(); // Phase 2 if (d < D && l < L_out) { int c_limit = min(C_TILE, C - c_start); const float* smem_row = smem + (l_local * SMEM_PITCH); const float* w_ptr = w_p_t + (c_start * D + d); for (int i = 0; i < c_limit; ++i) { float m = smem_row[i]; float w = w_ptr[i * D]; acc += w * m; } } __syncthreads(); } if (d < D && l < L_out) { if (bias_p != nullptr) acc += bias_p[d]; y[((b * D + d) * L_out + l)] = acc; } } ``` Wait, `tid = d_local * 32 + l_local`. `c_idx = tid / 32 = d_local`. `l_idx = tid % 32 = l_local`. So we don't need `tid`, `c_idx`, `l_idx`. We can directly use `d_local` and `l_local`. But note: In phase 1, we compute M for channel c = c_start + d_local. This means each block processes exactly D_TILE channels per C chunk. But D_TILE = 32. If D_TILE != C_TILE, that's okay. Here D_TILE = 32, C_TILE = 32. So each warp computes M for one channel (c = c_start + d_local). Since there are 32 warps (1024 threads / 32 = 32 warps), each warp handles one channel and all 32 l positions. That's efficient. But wait: if D is less than D_TILE (e.g., D=16), then some warps have d_local >= D. Then c = c_start + d_local may be valid if C is larger. But we only need M for channels up to C-1. However, some warps with d_local >= D will compute M for channels c >= C (if c_start + d_local >= C). Those M values are not needed for pointwise because d is invalid. However, they might be needed if C > D? Yes, M is computed for all c in chunk, regardless of d. But we assign M computation to d_local threads. If D=16 and C=256, then in a block we have only 16 valid d channels, but we have 32 warps. The extra 16 warps compute M for the extra 16 channels in the chunk. That's fine. But what if D=16 and blockDim.y = 32? Then gridDim.x = 1. Block has 32 d_local values. The extra d_local (16..31) compute M for channels c_start+16..c_start+31. Those M values are indeed needed by the pointwise computation for the valid d's (0..15). Because pointwise sums over all c. So M for all c in chunk must be computed. Since we have 32 warps, each computes one c, covering the full C_TILE=32 chunk. Good! So even if D < D_TILE, the block still has enough warps to compute all C_TILE channels, because we use d_local to index c. This is valid as long as D_TILE >= C_TILE or at least the number of warps equals C_TILE. Wait number of warps = blockDim.y * blockDim.x / 32 = (D_TILE * L_TILE) / 32. If D_TILE=32, L_TILE=32, we have 32 warps. Each warp computes one c (c_idx = warp_id). So we can compute up to 32 c per chunk. That's exactly C_TILE=32. So this works beautifully: the block computes M for C_TILE channels using the warps derived from d_local. Thus phase 1 doesn't need `tid` mapping; it's natural: thread with `d_local` computes M for channel `c = c_start + d_local`, across `l_local`. This is perfect. But what about the case where `C < C_TILE`? For the last chunk, c_start + d_local may exceed C-1 for large d_local. The condition `c < C` handles it by writing 0. Good. Similarly, `d < D` check in phase 2 ensures only valid d accumulate. So the kernel is robust. Now, one subtlety: The number of warps is D_TILE (since L_TILE=32). If D_TILE=32, warps=32. If D_TILE=64, warps=64, but block threads would be 64*32=2048 > 1024, not allowed. So D_TILE=32 is good. What if C is not a multiple of 32? The last chunk will have `c_limit = C - c_start < 32`. In phase 2, we loop `i < c_limit`. In smem, rows for extra c (i >= c_limit) contain 0 because phase 1 wrote 0 for `c >= C`. So we could just loop over the full C_TILE and avoid dynamic limit; but that would do extra work (multiply by 0). Multiplication by 0 is cheap but may still cost cycles. Dynamic limit with min is fine. But if we want to unroll the inner loop for performance, dynamic limit prevents unrolling. Since C is typically a multiple of 32? Not guaranteed, but we can handle with `c_limit`. For the evaluation, C=256 which is multiple of 32. We could also add a `if (C % 32 == 0)` fast path, but it's overkill. Now, consider the `w_p_t` pointer indexing. `w_ptr = w_p_t + (c_start * D + d)`. Then `w_ptr[i * D]`. Since `D` may not be known at compile time (but passed as argument), compiler can't fully unroll, but it's okay. Could we transpose `w_p_t` differently to have the inner loop over contiguous memory with stride 1? If we layout weight as [D, C] but want contiguous across D for fixed c, we need [C, D] which we have. The inner loop increments `i*D`, which is a strided access per thread, but all threads read same address per iteration (broadcast). Wait no: `w_ptr = w_p_t + (c_start * D + d)`. For iteration i, address = `w_p_t + (c_start + i)*D + d`. Threads in warp have same d, same i. So they all read the same address. That's a broadcast, not coalesced across threads. But broadcast is okay; shared memory / L1 handles it. It might cause a little serialization but it's reading same cache line. Actually when all threads in warp read the same address, it's broadcast from L1, very fast. So weight reads are optimal. Similarly, reading M from smem for fixed i and varying l: `smem[l * 33 + i]`. Threads in warp have varying l and same i. They read contiguous addresses in shared memory (since l varies, but pitch=33 ensures no bank conflict). That's fast. Overall, the kernel should be highly efficient. One more nuance: using `__restrict__` on pointers helps compiler optimize. Now, let's write the host code. C++ host wrapper: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> // forward declaration of kernel void fused_conv1d_kernel(const float* x, const float* w_d, const float* bias_d, const float* w_p_t, const float* bias_p, int B, int C, int D, int L_in, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, float* y); torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding) { // Ensure tensors are contiguous and on CUDA x = x.contiguous(); w_d = w_d.contiguous(); w_p_t = w_p_t.contiguous(); int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L_in = x.size(2); int K = w_d.size(1); // after squeeze: [C, K] int D = w_p_t.size(1); // [C, D] int L_out = (L_in + 2 * padding - K) / stride + 1; auto y = torch::empty({B, D, L_out}, x.options()); const int D_TILE = 32; const int L_TILE = 32; dim3 block(L_TILE, D_TILE); dim3 grid((D + D_TILE - 1) / D_TILE, (L_out + L_TILE - 1) / L_TILE, B); fused_conv1d_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), w_d.data_ptr<float>(), (bias_d.defined() && bias_d.numel() > 0) ? bias_d.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, w_p_t.data_ptr<float>(), (bias_p.defined() && bias_p.numel() > 0) ? bias_p.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, B, C, D, L_in, L_out, K, (int)stride, (int)padding, y.data_ptr<float>() ); return y; } ``` Wait, `bias_d` and `bias_p` might be undefined. In C++, passing an undefined tensor by reference is tricky. When calling from Python, if we pass `torch.empty(0)`, it's defined but numel=0. If we pass `torch.Tensor()` (undefined), it's undefined. In our Python forward, we can pass empty defined tensor or `torch.Tensor()`? If we pass None from Python to a pybind11 function taking `torch::Tensor`, it creates an undefined tensor. That is allowed; `defined()` returns false. So the wrapper above is fine. Alternatively, we can make the Python forward pass the raw Module parameters, and if bias is None, create an empty tensor. Let's decide later. Now, CUDA source: ``` #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <torch/extension.h> #define D_TILE 32 #define L_TILE 32 #define C_TILE 32 #define SMEM_PITCH (C_TILE + 1) __global__ void fused_conv1d_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w_d, const float* __restrict__ bias_d, const float* __restrict__ w_p_t, const float* __restrict__ bias_p, int B, int C, int D, int L_in, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, float* __restrict__ y) { __shared__ float smem[L_TILE * SMEM_PITCH]; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_d = blockIdx.x * D_TILE; int tile_l = blockIdx.y * L_TILE; int l_local = threadIdx.x; int d_local = threadIdx.y; int d = tile_d + d_local; int l = tile_l + l_local; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_start = 0; c_start < C; c_start += C_TILE) { int c = c_start + d_local; int l_m = tile_l + l_local; float m_val = 0.0f; if (c < C && l_m < L_out) { if (bias_d != nullptr) { m_val = bias_d[c]; } const float* x_ptr = x + ((b * C + c) * L_in); const float* w_ptr = w_d + (c * K); for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int l_in = l_m * stride + k - padding; float xv = 0.0f; if (l_in >= 0 && l_in < L_in) { xv = x_ptr[l_in]; } m_val += w_ptr[k] * xv; } } smem[l_local * SMEM_PITCH + d_local] = m_val; __syncthreads(); if (d < D && l < L_out) { int c_limit = C_TILE; if (c_start + C_TILE > C) { c_limit = C - c_start; } const float* smem_row = smem + (l_local * SMEM_PITCH); const float* w_ptr = w_p_t + (c_start * D + d); for (int i = 0; i < c_limit; ++i) { float m = smem_row[i]; float w = w_ptr[i * D]; acc += w * m; } } __syncthreads(); } if (d < D && l < L_out) { if (bias_p != nullptr) { acc += bias_p[d]; } y[((b * D + d) * L_out + l)] = acc; } } ``` Wait, in phase 2, `c_limit` calculation: `if (c_start + C_TILE > C) c_limit = C - c_start; else c_limit = C_TILE;`. We can write `int c_limit = min(C_TILE, C - c_start);`. But `C - c_start` is always positive inside loop. So `min(C_TILE, C - c_start)` is fine. One issue: In phase 1, we write `smem[l_local * SMEM_PITCH + d_local]`. Since d_local is the same as c_idx (0..31), and l_local is 0..31. Good. In phase 2, we read `smem_row[i]` where i goes from 0..c_limit-1. The address is `l_local * SMEM_PITCH + i`. Since SMEM_PITCH = 33, banks = (l_local*33 + i) % 32 = (l_local + i) % 32. For a warp, l_local varies (0..31), i is same. So banks vary -> no conflict. Good. Now, is there any case where `d_local >= C`? For c_start=0, c = d_local. If C < D_TILE (e.g., C=16, D=512), then for d_local >= 16, c >= C. Phase 1 writes 0. Phase 2: d = tile_d + d_local. Since D is large, d may be valid for d_local < D. But d_local from 16 to 31 will compute M for c=16..31, which are invalid (C=16), so they write 0. In phase 2, they read those 0s. That's fine. However, we are wasting warps computing M for invalid channels. But C is usually >= D_TILE? Not necessarily. In our case C=256 > 32. But if C is small (e.g., 8), then many warps are idle in phase 1. However, phase 2 still needs to sum over c_limit = min(32, C) = 8. The loop runs 8 times. The idle warps just write 0 and sync. Performance might degrade for small C, but it's okay. Could we instead set blockDim.y = min(D_TILE, D) or something? No, block dimensions are fixed at compile time. So we accept this. Now, what about the case where `d_local` is used to index c in phase 1, but `d_local` only goes up to 31, and C=256. We have 32 warps, each handling one c per chunk. Since C_TILE=32, each block handles 32 c's at a time. After finishing the chunk, it loops to next chunk. Good. Now, one more important thing: the kernel as written uses `float` exclusively. The problem says PyTorch reference uses fp32. Input is `torch.randn(...)`, default dtype float32. So it's fine. We need to handle cases where `stride != 1`, `padding` arbitrary. Our kernel supports generic stride and padding. One more optimization: we can precompute `l_in` start for the whole tile to avoid some computation, but not necessary. Another optimization: Since K is small, we can store `w_ptr[k]` in registers via a small array or just load from global each time. Since all threads in warp read same weight, it's cached. Loading from global each iteration is fine. Also, we should add `#pragma unroll` hints where possible. For the K loop, maybe `#pragma unroll 9` if K is known? But K is runtime variable. We can add `#pragma unroll` but compiler might ignore if not constant. Actually we can pass K as template parameter if we want to unroll, but that would require generating code for each K. Not necessary. But we can add `if (K == 9) { /* manually unrolled? */ }` using a switch, but that adds code bloat. Maybe not needed. One more thing: The wrapper uses `fused_conv1d_kernel<<<grid, block>>>` but it's declared as a function returning void. In C++ code, we need to declare it before use. The declaration can be in the `cpp_sources` or in `cuda_sources`. Actually we can put everything in a single string passed to `cuda_sources`? Let's do: `cpp_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> void fused_conv1d_kernel(const float* x, const float* w_d, const float* bias_d, const float* w_p_t, const float* bias_p, int B, int C, int D, int L_in, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, float* y); It's fine to declare as a regular function returning void. The compiler will match it with the `__global__` definition. However, `cpp_sources` is compiled by C++ compiler (not nvcc). It cannot parse `<<< >>>`. That's a problem! Ah, right. `cpp_sources` is compiled as C++ by the host compiler. However, to be absolutely safe, we can define a thin wrapper in `cpp_sources` that calls a CUDA launcher function declared in `cpp_sources` and defined in `cuda_sources`. But that adds complexity. The simplest is to put the host function (with `<<< >>>`) inside `cuda_sources`, and in `cpp_sources` have a declaration. Let me check documentation of `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`: From PyTorch docs: ``` torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline(name, cpp_sources, cuda_sources=None, functions=None, extra_cuda_cflags=None, extra_cflags=None, verbose=False, with_cuda=None, is_python_module=True, keep_intermediates=True) ``` - `cpp_sources`: string or list of strings containing C++ source code. - `cuda_sources`: similar, but for CUDA code. - `functions`: list of function names for which to generate Python bindings. The code is compiled with `cpp_extension.load`. The `cpp_sources` are compiled as standard C++, and `cuda_sources` with nvcc. The object files are linked. So yes, we can declare the function in `cpp_sources` and define it in `cuda_sources`. But sometimes there are issues with `torch::Tensor` in `cuda_sources` if not including proper headers. We will include `<torch/extension.h>`. Let's write the Python file accordingly. Wait, does `cpp_sources` need to have the `#include` for the declaration? Yes, we need to include `<torch/extension.h>` for `torch::Tensor` type. So `cpp_sources` will have: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding); ``` And `cuda_sources` will have the actual implementation of `forward` and the kernel. Actually we need to be careful: `functions=["forward"]` will generate a wrapper. If we declare `forward` in `cpp_sources` and define it in `cuda_sources`, the name `forward` is used for both. That should be okay. But if `cpp_sources` contains only the declaration, `load_inline` will generate binding code that needs to call `forward`. Since it's just a declaration, compilation of `cpp_sources` will succeed (assuming binding code includes the correct headers). Then linking with `cuda_sources` will resolve it. But I recall that `load_inline` might expect the functions to be defined somewhere accessible. It should work. However, sometimes it's easier to define the launcher as a regular function in `cuda_sources`, and call it from a host function in `cpp_sources`. Let's do that to avoid any ambiguity. Define in `cuda_sources`: ```cpp // kernel definition // launcher torch::Tensor forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding) { // ... allocate, launch kernel ... return y; } ``` Then in `cpp_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor forward_cuda(...); torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding) { return forward_cuda(x, w_d, bias_d, w_p_t, bias_p, stride, padding); } ``` But this is redundant. But it ensures the function callable from Python is defined in `cpp_sources`. However, `forward_cuda` is defined in `cuda_sources`. This is robust. Wait, but the binding for `forward` is generated. `forward` calls `forward_cuda`. The linker will resolve `forward_cuda` from `cuda_sources`. This should be fine. Alternatively, we can put everything in `cuda_sources` and just have a declaration in `cpp_sources`. But maybe it's safer to have the Python-exposed function in `cpp_sources`. Let's do the separate launcher. Actually, I just realized: `load_inline` generates the pybind11 bindings in `cpp_sources`. The generated code will contain something like: ```cpp m.def("forward", &forward, ...); ``` So `forward` must be declared before the generated code. If we define `forward` in `cpp_sources`, it's straightforward. If we declare in `cpp_sources` and define in `cuda_sources`, it's also fine. Let's keep it simple: declare and define `forward` in `cpp_sources`, but call a CUDA helper defined in `cuda_sources` to do the launch. Or define everything in `cuda_sources` using `extern "C"`? Hmm. I think the cleanest is: `cuda_sources`: kernel + a helper function `launch_fused_conv1d(...)` that does the launch and returns tensor. `cpp_sources`: include declaration of `launch_fused_conv1d`, define `forward` that calls it. `functions = ["forward"]`. This avoids CUDA syntax in `cpp_sources`. Let's implement: `cpp_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor launch_fused_conv1d(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding); torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding) { return launch_fused_conv1d(x, w_d, bias_d, w_p_t, bias_p, stride, padding); } ``` `cuda_sources`: ```cuda #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <torch/extension.h> #define D_TILE 32 #define L_TILE 32 #define C_TILE 32 #define SMEM_PITCH (C_TILE + 1) __global__ void fused_conv1d_kernel(...) { // ... } torch::Tensor launch_fused_conv1d(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding) { // ... call kernel ... return y; } ``` This is clean. Now, let's write the complete Python file. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <torch/extension.h> #define D_TILE 32 #define L_TILE 32 #define C_TILE 32 #define SMEM_PITCH (C_TILE + 1) __global__ void fused_conv1d_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w_d, const float* __restrict__ bias_d, const float* __restrict__ w_p_t, const float* __restrict__ bias_p, int B, int C, int D, int L_in, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, float* __restrict__ y) { __shared__ float smem[L_TILE * SMEM_PITCH]; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_d = blockIdx.x * D_TILE; int tile_l = blockIdx.y * L_TILE; int l_local = threadIdx.x; int d_local = threadIdx.y; int d = tile_d + d_local; int l = tile_l + l_local; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_start = 0; c_start < C; c_start += C_TILE) { int c = c_start + d_local; int l_m = tile_l + l_local; float m_val = 0.0f; if (c < C && l_m < L_out) { if (bias_d != nullptr) { m_val = bias_d[c]; } const float* x_ptr = x + ((b * C + c) * L_in); const float* w_ptr = w_d + (c * K); for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int l_in = l_m * stride + k - padding; float xv = 0.0f; if (l_in >= 0 && l_in < L_in) { xv = x_ptr[l_in]; } m_val += w_ptr[k] * xv; } } smem[l_local * SMEM_PITCH + d_local] = m_val; __syncthreads(); if (d < D && l < L_out) { int c_limit = C_TILE; if (c_start + C_TILE > C) { c_limit = C - c_start; } const float* smem_row = smem + (l_local * SMEM_PITCH); const float* w_ptr = w_p_t + (c_start * D + d); for (int i = 0; i < c_limit; ++i) { float m = smem_row[i]; float w = w_ptr[i * D]; acc += w * m; } } __syncthreads(); } if (d < D && l < L_out) { if (bias_p != nullptr) { acc += bias_p[d]; } y[((b * D + d) * L_out + l)] = acc; } } torch::Tensor launch_fused_conv1d(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding) { x = x.contiguous(); w_d = w_d.contiguous(); w_p_t = w_p_t.contiguous(); int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L_in = x.size(2); int K = w_d.size(1); int D = w_p_t.size(1); int L_out = (L_in + 2 * padding - K) / stride + 1; auto y = torch::empty({B, D, L_out}, x.options()); dim3 block(L_TILE, D_TILE); dim3 grid((D + D_TILE - 1) / D_TILE, (L_out + L_TILE - 1) / L_TILE, B); fused_conv1d_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), w_d.data_ptr<float>(), (bias_d.defined() && bias_d.numel() > 0) ? bias_d.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, w_p_t.data_ptr<float>(), (bias_p.defined() && bias_p.numel() > 0) ? bias_p.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, B, C, D, L_in, L_out, K, static_cast<int>(stride), static_cast<int>(padding), y.data_ptr<float>() ); return y; } ''' cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor launch_fused_conv1d(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding); torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding) { return launch_fused_conv1d(x, w_d, bias_d, w_p_t, bias_p, stride, padding); } ''' fused_conv = load_inline( name="fused_depthwise_pointwise_conv1d", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, bias: bool = False): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.stride = stride self.padding = padding self.bias = bias self.depthwise = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, in_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, groups=in_channels, bias=bias) self.pointwise = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, bias=bias) def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: # Prepare weights w_d = self.depthwise.weight.squeeze(1).contiguous() # [C, K] w_p = self.pointwise.weight.squeeze(2).t().contiguous() # [C, D] bias_d = self.depthwise.bias if self.depthwise.bias is not None else torch.empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) bias_p = self.pointwise.bias if self.pointwise.bias is not None else torch.empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) return fused_conv.forward(x, w_d, bias_d, w_p, bias_p, self.stride, self.padding) ``` This looks correct. But we need to think about the `load_inline` invocation. Since it's at module level, it will compile at import time. If there are compilation errors, we'll see them. We should test compile. Potential issues: - `empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype)` creates a defined tensor with size 0. In C++, `bias_d.defined()` is true, `numel() > 0` is false, so pointer is null. Good. - `w_d = self.depthwise.weight.squeeze(1)` might fail if weight is not contiguous or has weird strides. But `self.depthwise.weight` is newly initialized, so it's contiguous. But to be safe, we call `.contiguous()`. - Same for `w_p`. - Input `x` might not be contiguous. In the wrapper, we call `x.contiguous()`. Good. One performance note: calling `.contiguous()` and `.t().contiguous()` every forward might allocate memory and cause overhead. But weight sizes are small (512*256=131k floats = 0.5MB). The overhead is negligible compared to the kernel runtime. However, for repeated forward passes, we'd want to precompute the transposed weight. Since the evaluator likely runs forward once or a few times, it's okay. But we can store `w_p_t` as a precomputed buffer. To optimize, we can precompute the transposed weight in `__init__`. But `self.pointwise.weight` is a parameter; if we create a buffer `self.w_p_t`, it won't be updated during training. However, evaluation is static. To be safe, we can compute it in `forward` each time. But we can also do: in `__init__`, after creating `self.pointwise`, we could register a buffer: ```python w_p_t = self.pointwise.weight.detach().squeeze(2).t().contiguous() self.register_buffer('w_p_t', w_p_t) ``` But if `self.pointwise.weight` is updated by optimizer, `self.w_p_t` won't be updated. So this breaks training. Since the problem only says replace forward computation, and evaluation likely loads weights from a reference model (copying parameters), we don't need to worry about training. But to be robust, perhaps computing in forward is safer. Alternatively, we can compute in forward once and cache if not training? But we don't know if training is needed. The safe approach: compute in `forward` each time. The overhead is small. But we can add a check: if the weight is contiguous and already transposed? No. Actually, we can store the transposed weight as a parameter that shares storage with the original but is a view. For example: ```python # In __init__ self.pointwise = nn.Conv1d(...) with torch.no_grad(): self.w_p_t = nn.Parameter(self.pointwise.weight.squeeze(2).t().contiguous()) ``` But then we have two parameters pointing to same data? If we create a new Parameter, it's a copy. And the optimizer would update both, or we'd need to exclude one. Not worth it. Given that weight transpose overhead is tiny, we can just do it in forward. Wait, but `load_inline` will compile the extension at module load time. The name "fused_depthwise_pointwise_conv1d" might conflict if run multiple times? `load_inline` caches based on source code, so it's fine. Now, we should run compile and correctness tests. Let's do it. But before that, let's think about potential off-by-one errors. Input length: L_in = seq_len = 4096. Padding = 4, K=9, stride=1. L_out = (4096 + 2*4 - 9)/1 + 1 = (4096 + 8 - 9) + 1 = 4096. Good. In kernel: For l_m = tile_l + l_local. l_in = l_m * stride + k - padding. For l_m=0, k=0 -> l_in = -4 -> invalid, xval=0. l_m=0, k=4 -> l_in=0 -> valid. l_m=4095, k=8 -> l_in=4095+8-4=4099 -> invalid. So padding is symmetric? With K=9 and padding=4, yes: left pad 4, right pad 4. The kernel correctly handles bounds with `if (l_in >= 0 && l_in < L_in)`. Depthwise weight shape: after squeeze, [C, K]. w_ptr[k] = w_d[c, k]. Pointwise weight shape: after squeeze and transpose, [C, D]. w_ptr[i*D] = w_p_t[c_start + i, d]. Bias shapes: bias_d [C], bias_p [D]. Output shape: [B, D, L_out]. All good. One more thought: the kernel uses `const int D_TILE = 32;` as a define. If D is not a multiple of 32, some blocks have d >= D. In phase 2, those threads skip. In phase 1, they still compute M for c = c_start + d_local. But d_local can be up to 31. If C < c_start + 32, some of those c's are invalid and write 0. That's fine. But what if C > 32 and d_local corresponds to a valid c, but d >= D? Then M is computed and stored, and pointwise accumulation is skipped. That's fine. Similarly for l: if L_out not multiple of 32, some threads have l >= L_out. Phase 1: l_m >= L_out -> write 0. Phase 2: skip accumulation and write. Good. Now, let's consider if `stride > 1`. Then L_out = (L_in + 2*P - K)/stride + 1. Kernel uses `l_m * stride + k - padding`. That's correct. One issue: the type of `stride` and `padding` in kernel is `int`. `L_out` calculation in C++ uses integer arithmetic: `(L_in + 2 * padding - K) / stride + 1`. In C++, if stride doesn't divide evenly, this is integer division (floor). That matches PyTorch formula: `out_len = floor((L + 2*P - K)/S) + 1`. Actually PyTorch's conv1d formula is exactly that. We should also check if `L_in + 2*P - K` could be negative. The problem probably ensures valid parameters. But we could add a max with 0. Now, we should compile and test. Let's write the code string properly. In the Python source, we need to embed the C++ and CUDA strings. Use triple quotes. Then call `compile_kernel` first. But the tool `compile_kernel` expects the full Python source of `ModelNew`. It will compile and check for syntax errors etc. Let's write the full source and call compile_kernel. Actually, `compile_kernel` compiles the kernel without running it. It returns compiler output on failure. Good for catching CUDA compile errors. Let's create the full Python source. We'll need to be careful with string escaping in the triple quotes. Let's draft it: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <torch/extension.h> #define D_TILE 32 #define L_TILE 32 #define C_TILE 32 #define SMEM_PITCH (C_TILE + 1) __global__ void fused_conv1d_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ w_d, const float* __restrict__ bias_d, const float* __restrict__ w_p_t, const float* __restrict__ bias_p, int B, int C, int D, int L_in, int L_out, int K, int stride, int padding, float* __restrict__ y) { __shared__ float smem[L_TILE * SMEM_PITCH]; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_d = blockIdx.x * D_TILE; int tile_l = blockIdx.y * L_TILE; int l_local = threadIdx.x; int d_local = threadIdx.y; int d = tile_d + d_local; int l = tile_l + l_local; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_start = 0; c_start < C; c_start += C_TILE) { int c = c_start + d_local; int l_m = tile_l + l_local; float m_val = 0.0f; if (c < C && l_m < L_out) { if (bias_d != nullptr) { m_val = bias_d[c]; } const float* x_ptr = x + ((b * C + c) * L_in); const float* w_ptr = w_d + (c * K); for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int l_in = l_m * stride + k - padding; float xv = 0.0f; if (l_in >= 0 && l_in < L_in) { xv = x_ptr[l_in]; } m_val += w_ptr[k] * xv; } } smem[l_local * SMEM_PITCH + d_local] = m_val; __syncthreads(); if (d < D && l < L_out) { int c_limit = C_TILE; if (c_start + C_TILE > C) { c_limit = C - c_start; } const float* smem_row = smem + (l_local * SMEM_PITCH); const float* w_ptr = w_p_t + (c_start * D + d); for (int i = 0; i < c_limit; ++i) { float m = smem_row[i]; float w = w_ptr[i * D]; acc += w * m; } } __syncthreads(); } if (d < D && l < L_out) { if (bias_p != nullptr) { acc += bias_p[d]; } y[((b * D + d) * L_out + l)] = acc; } } torch::Tensor launch_fused_conv1d(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding) { x = x.contiguous(); w_d = w_d.contiguous(); w_p_t = w_p_t.contiguous(); int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L_in = x.size(2); int K = w_d.size(1); int D = w_p_t.size(1); int L_out = (L_in + 2 * padding - K) / stride + 1; auto y = torch::empty({B, D, L_out}, x.options()); dim3 block(L_TILE, D_TILE); dim3 grid((D + D_TILE - 1) / D_TILE, (L_out + L_TILE - 1) / L_TILE, B); fused_conv1d_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), w_d.data_ptr<float>(), (bias_d.defined() && bias_d.numel() > 0) ? bias_d.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, w_p_t.data_ptr<float>(), (bias_p.defined() && bias_p.numel() > 0) ? bias_p.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, B, C, D, L_in, L_out, K, static_cast<int>(stride), static_cast<int>(padding), y.data_ptr<float>() ); return y; } """ cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor launch_fused_conv1d(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding); torch::Tensor forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_d, torch::Tensor bias_d, torch::Tensor w_p_t, torch::Tensor bias_p, int64_t stride, int64_t padding) { return launch_fused_conv1d(x, w_d, bias_d, w_p_t, bias_p, stride, padding); } """ fused_conv = load_inline( name="fused_depthwise_pointwise_conv1d", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, bias: bool = False): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.depthwise = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, in_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, groups=in_channels, bias=bias) self.pointwise = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, bias=bias) self.stride = stride self.padding = padding def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: w_d = self.depthwise.weight.squeeze(1).contiguous() w_p = self.pointwise.weight.squeeze(2).t().contiguous() bias_d = self.depthwise.bias if self.depthwise.bias is not None else torch.empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) bias_p = self.pointwise.bias if self.pointwise.bias is not None else torch.empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) return fused_conv.forward(x, w_d, bias_d, w_p, bias_p, self.stride, self.padding) ``` Let's compile it.

It seems we have a runtime import failure due to a missing CXXABI version. Actually, standard dynamic linker behavior: if `libstdc++.so.6` is already loaded in the global scope, the symbols will be resolved from that object, even if the new `.so` has its own `NEEDED` entry and `RPATH`. But if the already loaded object does not provide the versioned symbol (`CXXABI_1.3.15`), then resolving will fail. The only ways around this are: 1) `dlmopen` to a new namespace (not easy), 2) `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` before starting the process (too late), 3) static linking of libstdc++. Then the `.so` will embed the C++ standard library code and won't need the dynamic `libstdc++` at runtime, avoiding the version mismatch. Depthwise weight w_d: (C, 1, K) -> we will squeeze to (C, K) 3. Pointwise weight w_p: (D, C, 1) -> we will transpose to (C, D) or something? Wait, pointwise is linear combination across channels for each position. It mixes channels. `nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=1, bias=bias)` uses weight shape `(out_channels, in_channels, 1)`. In PyTorch, conv1d is `out[b, j, l] = sum_i sum_k weight[j, i, k] * input[b, i, l + k - padding]`. For pointwise with kernel_size=1, this reduces to `out[b, j, l] = sum_i weight[j, i, 0] * input[b, i, l]`. So it's just a matrix multiplication across channels at each position. For the fusion, we can compute the output as: `y[b, d, l] = sum_c w_p[d, c] * ( bias_d[c] + sum_k w_d[c, k] * x[b, c, l*stride + k - padding] ) + bias_p[d]`. Since `stride=1, padding=4, K=9`. The total operations per output element: - Depthwise: for each c, l: K=9 mul-adds. - Pointwise: for each d, l: C=256 mul-adds. Total per (d,l): C*D*(K+1) ≈ 256*512*10 = 1.3M mul-adds per batch element? Wait, B=32, D=512, L_out=4096. Total output elements: 32 * 512 * 4096 = 67M. Depthwise compute per l: C*K = 256*9 = 2304 per position. Pointwise compute per l: C*D = 256*512 = 131k per position. Total per position: ~133k ops. Total across all positions: 133k * 4096 * 32 = 17.4B ops. That's a lot. It's compute bound if we can read the data efficiently. But memory bandwidth: reading x once: B*C*L_in = 32*256*4096 = 33.5M floats = 134MB. Reading w_d: C*K = 2.3KB. Pointwise weights: D*C = 131KB. Reading x dominates. Writing y: B*D*L_out = 32*512*4096 = 268MB. Total memory traffic ~400MB. At 1 TB/s, kernel would take 400us just for memory. The compute is 17.4 GFLOPs. At 10 TFLOPS, that's 1.7ms. So compute dominates. Thus we should focus on maximizing compute throughput with fused kernels and good occupancy. Fusing both operations into a single kernel allows us to compute depthwise on the fly and feed directly into pointwise accumulation, without writing intermediate activations to DRAM. This reduces memory bandwidth by a factor of around 3 (since the intermediate tensor after depthwise would be B*C*L_out = 134MB write and read). Fusing saves ~268MB of DRAM traffic. However, naive fusion as in my second attempt uses only shared memory for a single C tile but computes depthwise inside the kernel on registers, which is fine. The issue is that the kernel design had each thread computing one output element (d, l). The inner loop was over c. For each c, it computes the depthwise convolution (k loop of size 9) and loads w_p_t. This is a sequential loop over c. Since C=256, and the computation per thread is high, this might be acceptable, but occupancy might be low because each thread uses many registers. Let's estimate: `block = (TILE_L=64, TILE_D=16)` => 1024 threads per block. This is the max for many GPUs. If we have D=512, L_out=4096, B=32, total threads = 512*4096*32 = 67M. With 1024 threads per block, and let's say 80 SMs, we can launch many blocks. But each thread does a loop of 256 * (9+1) = 2560 operations. Since there are 67M threads each doing 2560 FMAs, that's 17.4 GFLOPs. At 1.5 GHz and ~128 FMA per SM per clock (FP32), 80 SMs give 80*128*2*1.5 = 30.7 TFLOPS. So compute bound. Actually, using one thread per output element might be okay but there is a lot of redundant loading of `x`. Each thread loads K=9 elements of x for each c. Since different threads in the same block at the same c but different l will load overlapping x: for l and l+1, the loaded windows overlap by K-1=8. So memory locality is bad because each thread loads 9 elements independently. But the x for each channel c is the same across all d at the same l. Better kernel design: - Tiles over L (sequential) and D. - Load a tile of x into shared memory, compute the depthwise for that tile to get an intermediate M (C, tile_L). Since depthwise is per channel, it could be done efficiently by loading x window for the tile. For a tile of L size say 64, we need to load x elements for indices from `tile_l*stride - padding` to `(tile_l + TILE_L - 1)*stride + K - 1 - padding`. This is `TILE_L + K - 1` elements. For TILE_L=64, K=9, that's 72 floats per channel. - Then do the pointwise: for each d, compute sum over c of w_p[d,c] * M[c,l]. - Since C=256 and D=512, the pointwise is a matrix multiplication `M^T (L x C) @ W_p (C x D)`. We can use a GEMM-like approach. Given the sizes: B=32, L_out=4096, C=256, D=512. M shape after depthwise: (B, C, L_out). Pointwise is W_p: (D, C) or (C, D). So output Y = W_p @ M (with appropriate dims). That is `y[b,d,l] = sum_c w_p[d,c] * m[b,c,l]`. So we can think of it as: for each batch b, compute M[b] = depthwise(x[b]) and then Y[b] = W_p @ M[b]. This is a batched matrix multiplication where M is computed on the fly. But for a fused kernel, we can compute tiles of M in shared memory and then compute Y for a tile of D. A good approach: - Outer loops over batch b and tile_l. - For each tile_l (say 64), load the required x slice for all c into shared memory. Since x is (B, C, L_in), and C=256, we can load a (C x (tile_l + K - 1)) tile. That's 256 * 72 = 18432 floats = 72KB. This fits nicely in shared memory (up to ~48-100KB per SM). - Then compute the depthwise output for this tile: for each c and each l in tile, `m[c,l] = sum_k w_d[c,k] * x_sm[c, l*stride + k - padding + padding_offset]`. Since K=9, each thread could compute one (c, l) pair. There are 256*64 = 16384 such pairs. We can have threads process them. - After computing M tile, we compute Y tile: for a subset of D (say 64), and for all l in tile (64), `y[d,l] = sum_c w_p[d,c] * m[c,l]`. This is a GEMM-like operation of size (D_tile x L_tile) += (D_tile x C) @ (C x L_tile). Use shared memory for W_p tile and M tile. Since D=512, C=256, L_tile=64. W_p tile: D_tile (64) x C (256) = 16K floats = 64KB. M tile: C (256) x L_tile (64) = 16K floats = 64KB. Total shared memory ~ 72KB (x) + 64KB (W_p) + 64KB (M) = 200KB. That's too much for 48KB/shared memory bank. So we need smaller tiles. Let's compute with L_tile=32, D_tile=32, C_tile=32. X tile: C x (L_tile + K - 1) = 256 * 40 = 10240 = 40KB (if we load all C). Better fusion strategy: - Compute a tile of intermediate M = depthwise(x) for a tile of channels C_tile and a tile of spatial positions L_tile. - Store M tile in shared memory. - Compute a tile of output Y for D_tile from this M tile. - Iterate over C tiles, accumulating into registers. This way, depthwise is computed only once per C tile (not once per D). Since D=512 and C=256, we process all D at once per C tile. Specifically: - Block shape: `D_THREADS` x `L_THREADS`. Let's pick `L_TILE = 32` and `D_TILE = 32`. - Each block computes a tile of output: D in [tile_d, tile_d+32), L in [tile_l, tile_l+32), for a given batch b. - The block loads the pointwise weight tile for D_tile: `w_p[d, c_start:c_start+C_CHUNK]` into shared memory or registers. - It computes the intermediate `m[c,l]` for `c` in `c_start:c_start+C_CHUNK` and `l` in `L_TILE` by reading from x directly. - Then it accumulates `y[d,l] += w_p[d,c] * m[c,l]`. Since C=256, we can process C in chunks of say `C_TILE = 32`. For each `C_TILE`: 1. Load X window for all `c` in chunk: `C_TILE * (L_TILE + K - 1)` floats. To resolve this, we can transpose the thread mapping for the m computation: use warp shuffles or just let each thread compute m for a (c,l) pair derived from its (d,l) coordinates. Block shape (L=32, D=32). Warp = fixed d, varying l. For m computation, we assign `c_compute = d_local` (0..31) and `l_compute = l_local` (0..31). Then thread (d,l) computes m for channel c = c_start + d, position l. In a warp, d is fixed, so c_compute is fixed! That's perfect! All threads in a warp compute m for the same c and varying l. This means x loads are coalesced! And different warps compute different c. Since warps are for d=0..31, we cover c=0..31. Then each thread writes `m` to shared memory at `smem_m[d_local][l_local]` (since c_local = d_local). After `__syncthreads()`, the shared memory contains m for all c=0..31 and l=0..31. Then accumulation: each thread (d,l) needs to compute `acc += w_p[d_global][c_start + c] * smem_m[c][l]`. It loops over c=0..31, reading `smem_m[c][l]` (same l, varying c). Since `l` is constant for the thread, and `c` varies, the accesses to `smem_m` are within the same column l but different rows c. If `smem_m` is stored row-major (c * L_TILE + l), then accesses for varying c and fixed l are strided by L_TILE (32). That's fine because shared memory is not bank-conflicted as long as each thread accesses a different bank. Bank width on A100 is 4 bytes, 32 banks. Address of `smem_m[c][l]` = `c*32 + l`. For a warp of threads with varying d but same l, they are reading different c values? Wait, a warp is fixed d_local and varying l_local. But all threads in a warp have the same `l_local`? No, they have different `l_local` because warp = fixed d, varying l. During accumulation, each thread uses its own `l_local`. So in a warp, threads are accessing `smem_m[c][l]` with different `l`. The address for lane l is `c*32 + l`. For a fixed c, different l map to different banks (since stride 1). So no bank conflicts. Good. Then as we loop over c in the same thread, we access `c*32 + l`, `c*32 + l + 32`, etc. That's bank 0 if l is the same? Actually, `(c*32 + l) % 32 = l`. So for a fixed l, consecutive c accesses map to the same bank! That means if a thread loops over c, it will hit the same bank every time, which is fine because it's one thread. Bank conflicts occur when multiple threads in the same warp access the same bank simultaneously. Since each thread accesses its own bank (determined by l), they don't conflict. However, if two threads in the same warp have the same l_local, they would conflict. In a warp, `l_local` varies from 0 to 31 (if it is the x-dim). Thus each thread in the warp has a unique l_local and hence a unique bank. No bank conflicts. Perfect. But what about w_p? We need to read w_p_t for each c and d_local. The weight tile `w_p[d_global][c_start + c]` for d in the block. We can load it into shared memory or registers. If we keep it in registers, each thread needs 32 floats. Let's do shared memory for w_p as well. Load w_p tile into shared memory `smem_w[d_local][c_local]` (32x32 = 1024 floats). Each thread loads one element. How to map? - We can map `d_local = threadIdx.y`, `c_local = threadIdx.x`? But `threadIdx.x` is l_local. So thread (d,l) loads `w_p_t[(c_start + l) * D + d_global]` into `smem_w[d_local][l_local]`. So the algorithm for each C chunk (C_TILE = 32) is: ```cuda // Step 1: Load w_p tile for this C chunk into shared memory. // smem_w is 32 x 32 floats. // Thread (d_local, l_local) loads: int c_local = threadIdx.x; // using l index as c for loading int d_local = threadIdx.y; if (d_global < D && c_start + c_local < C) { smem_w[d_local * 32 + c_local] = w_p_t[(c_start + c_local) * D + d_global]; } else { smem_w[d_local * 32 + c_local] = 0.0f; } // Step 2: Compute depthwise intermediate m for this C chunk. // We assign c_compute = d_local, l_compute = l_local. int c_compute = d_local; int l_compute = l_local; float m_val = 0.0f; if (c_start + c_compute < C && l_compute < L_TILE) { int c_idx = c_start + c_compute; int l_out = tile_l + l_compute; if (bias_d) m_val = bias_d[c_idx]; for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int l_in = l_out * stride + k - padding; float xv = 0.0f; if (l_in >= 0 && l_in < L_in) { xv = x[b * C * L_in + c_idx * L_in + l_in]; } m_val += w_d[c_idx * K + k] * xv; } } smem_m[c_compute * 32 + l_compute] = m_val; __syncthreads(); // Step 3: Accumulate pointwise for output element (d_local, l_local) float acc = ...; int c_limit = min(C_TILE, C - c_start); for (int c = 0; c < c_limit; ++c) { float m = smem_m[c * 32 + l_local]; float w = smem_w[d_local * 32 + c]; acc += w * m; } ``` Wait, but note: in step 2, `c_compute = d_local`. Now, does the kernel avoid writing intermediate M to global memory? Yes. We need to think about launch configuration. Block size: (32, 32) = 1024 threads. Grid: ( (L_out+31)/32, (D+31)/32, B ) = (128, 16, 32). Total blocks = 65536. On A100 with 108 SMs, each SM gets ~600 blocks. That's plenty. Will this be faster than PyTorch? PyTorch likely uses cudnn/cudnn for conv1d, which for depthwise might use something like im2col + gemm, or specialized depthwise kernels. For pointwise (1x1), it's basically a GEMM. PyTorch will call cudnn for each conv separately. The fused kernel should be faster because: - It writes and reads M from shared memory instead of global memory. - The arithmetic intensity is high and compute bound. - PyTorch's two separate convolutions involve separate kernel launches and intermediate storage. But PyTorch's depthwise conv for groups=in_channels might not be highly optimized; it might fall back to a generic im2col algorithm, which would be memory-bound. The pointwise 1x1 conv is dispatched to cuBLAS GEMM, which is highly optimized but still requires reading the intermediate tensor. The fused kernel avoids that.
